   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Solomon Islands</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="contentSub2"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">
       Country in the southwestern Pacific
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       For the group of islands rather than the state, see <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_(archipelago)" title="Solomon Islands (archipelago)">Solomon Islands (archipelago)</a>.
      </div> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Salomon_Islands" title="Salomon Islands">Salomon Islands</a>.
      </div>

      <p><b>Solomon Islands</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a><sup id="cite_ref-eb_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eb-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dc_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dc-9">[9]</a></sup> consisting of six major islands and over 900 smaller islands in <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, to the east of <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> and northwest of <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a> and covering a land area of 28,400 square kilometres (11,000&nbsp;sq&nbsp;mi). The country has a population of 652,858<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> and its capital, <a href="/wiki/Honiara" title="Honiara">Honiara</a>, is located on the island of <a href="/wiki/Guadalcanal" title="Guadalcanal">Guadalcanal</a>. The country takes its name from <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_(archipelago)" title="Solomon Islands (archipelago)">the Solomon Islands archipelago</a>, which is a collection of <a href="/wiki/Melanesia" title="Melanesia">Melanesian</a> islands that also includes the <a href="/wiki/North_Solomon_Islands" title="North Solomon Islands">North Solomon Islands</a> (a part of <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>), but excludes outlying islands, such as <a href="/wiki/Rennell_and_Bellona_Province" title="Rennell and Bellona Province">Rennell and Bellona</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Cruz_Islands" title="Santa Cruz Islands">Santa Cruz Islands</a>. </p>
      <p>The islands have been settled since at least 30,000–28,800 BC, with later waves of migrants, notably the <a href="/wiki/Lapita_culture" title="Lapita culture">Lapita</a> people, mixing and producing the modern indigenous Solomon Islanders population. In 1568, the Spanish navigator <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a" class="mw-redirect" title="Álvaro de Mendaña">Álvaro de Mendaña</a> was the first European to visit them, naming them the <i>Islas Salomón</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_University_Library_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Library-11">[11]</a></sup> Mendaña returned decades later in 1595 and another Spanish expedition led by Portuguese navigator <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Fernandes_de_Queir%C3%B3s" title="Pedro Fernandes de Queirós">Pedro Fernandes de Queirós</a> visited the Solomons in 1606. Britain defined its area of interest in the Solomon Islands archipelago in June 1893, when Captain Gibson R.N., of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Curacoa_(1878)" title="HMS Curacoa (1878)">HMS&nbsp;<i>Curacoa</i></a>, declared the southern Solomon Islands a <a href="/wiki/British_protectorate" title="British protectorate">British protectorate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BRL6_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL6-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_campaign" title="Solomon Islands campaign">Solomon Islands campaign</a> (1942–1945) saw fierce fighting between the United States, Commonwealth forces and the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guadalcanal" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Guadalcanal">Battle of Guadalcanal</a>. </p>
      <p>The official name of the then-British administration was changed from the <a href="/wiki/British_Solomon_Islands" title="British Solomon Islands">British Solomon Islands Protectorate</a> to the Solomon Islands in 1975, and self-government was achieved the following year. Independence was obtained, and the name changed to just "Solomon Islands" (without the <a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">definite article</a>), in 1978. At independence, Solomon Islands became a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Solomon_Islands" title="Monarchy of Solomon Islands">Queen of Solomon Islands</a> is <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Elizabeth II</a>, represented by the <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_Solomon_Islands" title="Governor-General of Solomon Islands">Governor-General</a>. </p>

          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li> 
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">8.3</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a></li> 
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">8.4</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a></li> 
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Sport"><span class="tocnumber">8.5</span> <span class="toctext">Sport</span></a></li> 
         </ul> </li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li> 
       </ul> 
      </div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>In 1568, the Spanish navigator <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a" class="mw-redirect" title="Álvaro de Mendaña">Álvaro de Mendaña</a> was the first European to visit the Solomon Islands archipelago, naming it <i>Islas Salomón</i> ("Solomon Islands") after the wealthy <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">biblical</a> <a href="/wiki/King_Solomon" class="mw-redirect" title="King Solomon">King Solomon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_University_Library_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton_University_Library-11">[11]</a></sup> It is said that they were given this name in the mistaken assumption that they contained great riches,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and he believed them to be the Bible-mentioned city of <a href="/wiki/Ophir" title="Ophir">Ophir</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> During most of the colonial period, the territory's official name was "British Solomon Islands Protectorate" until 1975, when it was changed to "Solomon Islands".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The definite article, "the", is not part of the country's official name but is sometimes used, both within and outside the country. Colloquially the islands are referred to simply as "the Solomons".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Solomon_Islands" title="History of Solomon Islands">History of Solomon Islands</a>
      </div> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>The Solomons were first colonised by people coming from the <a href="/wiki/Bismarck_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Bismarck Islands">Bismarck Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> era circa 30,000–28,000 BC, based on archaeological evidence found at <a href="/wiki/Kilu_Cave" title="Kilu Cave">Kilu Cave</a> on <a href="/wiki/Buka_Island" title="Buka Island">Buka Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_Region_of_Bougainville" title="Autonomous Region of Bougainville">Autonomous Region of Bougainville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Review_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> At this point sea levels were lower and Buka and Bougainville were physically joined to the southern Solomons in one landmass ('Greater Bougainville'), though it is unclear precisely how far south these early settlers spread as no other archaeological site from this period have yet been found.<sup id="cite_ref-Review_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review-19">[19]</a></sup> As sea levels rose as the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Period" title="Last Glacial Period">Ice Age</a> ended circa 4000-3500 BC, the Greater Bougainville landmass split into the numerous islands that exist today.<sup id="cite_ref-Review_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Exploration_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exploration-21">[21]</a></sup> Evidence of later human settlements dating to c. 4500-2500 BC have been found at Poha Cave and Vatuluma Posovi Cave on <a href="/wiki/Guadalcanal" title="Guadalcanal">Guadalcanal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Review_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review-19">[19]</a></sup> The ethnographic identity of these early peoples is unclear, though it is thought that the speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Solomon_languages" title="Central Solomon languages">Central Solomon languages</a> (a self-contained language family unrelated to other languages spoken in the Solomons) likely represent the descendants of these earlier settlers. </p>
      <p>From c. 1200–800 BC <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian</a> <a href="/wiki/Lapita_culture" title="Lapita culture">Lapita</a> people began arriving from the Bismarcks with their characteristic <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Review_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Evidence for their presence has been across the Solomon archipelago, as well at the <a href="/wiki/Santa_Cruz_Islands" title="Santa Cruz Islands">Santa Cruz Islands</a> in the south-east, with different islands being settled at different times.<sup id="cite_ref-Review_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review-19">[19]</a></sup> Linguistic and genetic evidence suggests that the Lapita people "leap-frogged" the already inhabited main Solomon Islands and settled first on the Santa Cruz group, with later back-migrations bringing their culture to the main group.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> These peoples mixed with the native Solomon Islanders and over time their languages became dominant, with most of the 60–70 languages spoken there belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_languages" title="Oceanic languages">Oceanic</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_language_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Austronesian language family">Austronesian language family</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Then as now communities tended to exist in small villages practising subsistence agriculture, though extensive inter-island trade networks existed.<sup id="cite_ref-Review_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review-19">[19]</a></sup> Numerous ancient burial sites and other evidence of permanent settlements have been found from the period 1000–1500 AD throughout the islands, one of the most prominent examples being the Roviana cultural complex centred on the islands off the southern coast of <a href="/wiki/New_Georgia" title="New Georgia">New Georgia</a>, where a large number of megalithic shrines and other structures were constructed in the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Arrival_of_Europeans_.281568.E2.80.931886.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_of_Europeans_(1568–1886)">Arrival of Europeans (1568–1886)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Arrival of Europeans (1568–1886)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Alvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neyra.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Alvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neyra.png/180px-Alvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neyra.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Alvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neyra.png/270px-Alvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neyra.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/Alvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neyra.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="386"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Alvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neyra.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Álvaro de Mendaña de Neira (1542–1595), the first European to sight the Solomons
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The first European to visit the islands was the Spanish navigator <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_de_Menda%C3%B1a_de_Neira" title="Álvaro de Mendaña de Neira">Álvaro de Mendaña de Neira</a>, sailing from <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_Peru" title="Viceroyalty of Peru">Peru</a> in 1568.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup> Landing on <a href="/wiki/Santa_Isabel_Island" title="Santa Isabel Island">Santa Isabel</a> on 7 February, Mendaña explored several of the other islands including <a href="/wiki/Makira" title="Makira">Makira</a>, Guadalcanal and <a href="/wiki/Malaita" title="Malaita">Malaita</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup> Relations with the native Solomon Islanders were initially cordial, though often soured as time went by.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup> As a result, Mendaña returned to Peru in August 1568.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup> He returned to the Solomons with a larger crew on a second voyage in 1595, aiming to colonise the islands.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup> They landed on <a href="/wiki/Nend%C3%B6" class="mw-redirect" title="Nendö">Nendö</a> in the Santa Cruz Islands and established a small settlement at Gracioso Bay.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup> However the settlement failed due to poor relations with the native peoples and epidemics of disease amongst the Spanish which caused numerous deaths, with Mendaña himself dying in October.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup> The new commander <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Fernandes_de_Queir%C3%B3s" title="Pedro Fernandes de Queirós">Pedro Fernandes de Queirós</a> thus decided to abandon the settlement and they sailed north to the Spanish territory of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Princeton_27-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Princeton-27">[27]</a></sup> Queirós later returned to the area in 1606, where he sighted <a href="/wiki/Tikopia" title="Tikopia">Tikopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taumako" title="Taumako">Taumako</a>, though this voyage was primarily to Vanuatu in the search of <a href="/wiki/Terra_Australis" title="Terra Australis">Terra Australis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Save for <a href="/wiki/Abel_Tasman" title="Abel Tasman">Abel Tasman</a>'s sighting of the remote <a href="/wiki/Ontong_Java_Atoll" title="Ontong Java Atoll">Ontong Java Atoll</a> in 1648, no European sailed to the Solomons again until 1767, when the British explorer <a href="/wiki/Philip_Carteret" title="Philip Carteret">Philip Carteret</a> sailed by the Santa Cruz Islands, Malaita and, continuing further north, Bougainville and the Bismarck Islands.<sup id="cite_ref-Exploration_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exploration-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup> French explorers also reached the Solomons, with <a href="/wiki/Louis_Antoine_de_Bougainville" title="Louis Antoine de Bougainville">Louis Antoine de Bougainville</a> naming <a href="/wiki/Choiseul_Island" title="Choiseul Island">Choiseul</a> in 1768 and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois-Marie_de_Surville" title="Jean-François-Marie de Surville">Jean-François-Marie de Surville</a> exploring the islands in 1769.<sup id="cite_ref-Exploration_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exploration-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1788 <a href="/wiki/John_Shortland_(Royal_Navy_officer)" title="John Shortland (Royal Navy officer)">John Shortland</a>, captaining a supply ship for Britain's new <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> colony at <a href="/wiki/Botany_Bay" title="Botany Bay">Botany Bay</a>, sighted the <a href="/wiki/Treasury_Islands" title="Treasury Islands">Treasury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shortland_Islands" title="Shortland Islands">Shortland Islands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Exploration_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exploration-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup> That same year the French explorer <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_de_Galaup,_comte_de_La_P%C3%A9rouse" class="mw-redirect" title="Jean-François de Galaup, comte de La Pérouse">Jean-François de La Pérouse</a> was wrecked on <a href="/wiki/Vanikoro" title="Vanikoro">Vanikoro</a>; a rescue expedition led by <a href="/wiki/Bruni_d%27Entrecasteaux" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruni d'Entrecasteaux">Bruni d'Entrecasteaux</a> sailed to Vanikoro but found no trace of La Pérouse.<sup id="cite_ref-Exploration_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exploration-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NLA_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NLA-32">[32]</a></sup> The fate of La Pérouse was not confirmed until 1826, when the English merchant <a href="/wiki/Peter_Dillon" title="Peter Dillon">Peter Dillon</a> visited Tikopia and discovered items belonging to La Pérouse in the possession of the local people, confirmed by the subsequent voyage of <a href="/wiki/Jules_Dumont_d%27Urville" title="Jules Dumont d'Urville">Jules Dumont d'Urville</a> in 1828.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Some of the earliest regular foreign visitors to the islands were <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a> vessels from Britain, the United States and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> They came for food, wood and water from late in the 18th century, establishing a trading relationship with the Solomon Islanders and later taking aboard islanders to serve as crewmen on their ships.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Relations between the islanders and visiting seamen was not always good and sometimes there was bloodshed.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> A knock-on effect of the greater European contact was the spread of diseases to which local peoples had no immunity, as well a shift in the balance of power between coastal groups, who had access to European weapons and technology, and inland groups who did not.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup> In the second half of the 1800s more traders arrived seeking turtleshells, <a href="/wiki/Sea_cucumbers" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea cucumbers">sea cucumbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copra" title="Copra">copra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandalwood" title="Sandalwood">sandalwood</a>, occasionally establishing semi-permanent trading stations.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup> However initial attempts at more long-term settlement, such as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Boyd" title="Benjamin Boyd">Benjamin Boyd</a>'s colony on Guadalcanal in 1851, were unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg/220px-Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg/330px-Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg/440px-Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="485" data-file-height="336"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Solomon_Islands_canoe_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Solomon Island warriors, armed with spears, on board an ornamented war canoe (1895)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Beginning in the 1840s, and accelerating in the 1860s, islanders began to be recruited (or often kidnapped) as labourers for the colonies in Australia, Fiji and Samoa in a process known as "<a href="/wiki/Blackbirding" title="Blackbirding">blackbirding</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup> Conditions for workers were often poor and exploitative, and local islanders often <a href="/wiki/List_of_massacres_in_the_Solomon_Islands" title="List of massacres in the Solomon Islands">violently</a> attacked any Europeans who appeared on their island.<sup id="cite_ref-DRL_29-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DRL-29">[29]</a></sup> The blackbird trade was chronicled by prominent Western writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Joe_Melvin" title="Joe Melvin">Joe Melvin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_London" title="Jack London">Jack London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-adb_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adb-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CruiseSnark_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CruiseSnark-40">[40]</a></sup> Christian missionaries also began visiting the Solomons from the 1840s, beginning with an attempt by French Catholics under Jean-Baptiste Epalle to establish a mission on Santa Isabel, which was abandoned after Epalle was killed by islanders in 1845.<sup id="cite_ref-Exploration_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exploration-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup> Anglican missionaries began arriving from the 1850s, followed by other denominations, over time gaining a large number of converts.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Colonial_period_.281886.E2.80.931978.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_period_(1886–1978)">Colonial period (1886–1978)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Colonial period (1886–1978)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/British_Solomon_Islands" title="British Solomon Islands">British Solomon Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Western_Pacific_Territories" title="British Western Pacific Territories">British Western Pacific Territories</a>, and <a href="/wiki/German_New_Guinea" title="German New Guinea">German New Guinea</a>
      </div> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_colonial_rule">Establishment of colonial rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Establishment of colonial rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In 1884 <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a> annexed north-east New Guinea and the Bismarck Archipelago, and in 1886 they extended their rule over the <a href="/wiki/North_Solomon_Islands" title="North Solomon Islands">North Solomon Islands</a>, covering Bougainville, Buka, Choiseul, Santa Isabel, the Shortlands and Ontong Java atoll.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In 1886 Germany and Britain confirmed this arrangement, with Britain gaining a "sphere of influence" over the southern Solomons.<sup id="cite_ref-Sack_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sack-43">[43]</a></sup> Germany paid little attention to the islands, with German authorities based in New Guinea not even visiting the area until 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-Sack_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sack-43">[43]</a></sup> The German presence, along with pressure from the missionaries to rein in the excesses of the blackbirding system, prompted the British to declare a protectorate over the southern Solomons in March 1893, initially encompassing New Georgia, Malaita, Guadalcanal, Makira, Mono Island and the central Nggela Islands.<sup id="cite_ref-BRL6_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL6-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Proclamation_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Proclamation-44">[44]</a></sup> In April 1896 <a href="/wiki/Charles_Morris_Woodford" title="Charles Morris Woodford">Charles Morris Woodford</a> was appointed as the British Acting Deputy Commissioner and confirmed in post the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-BRL6_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL6-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Proclamation_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Proclamation-44">[44]</a></sup> Woodford set up an administrative headquarters on the small island of <a href="/wiki/Tulagi" title="Tulagi">Tulagi</a>, and in 1898 and 1899 the <a href="/wiki/Rennell_and_Bellona_Province" title="Rennell and Bellona Province">Rennell and Bellona Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikaiana" title="Sikaiana">Sikaiana</a>, the Santa Cruz Islands and outlying islands such as Anuta, Fataka, Temotu and Tikopia were added to the protectorate.<sup id="cite_ref-Proclamation_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Proclamation-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BRL7a_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL7a-45">[45]</a></sup> In 1900, under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Convention" title="Tripartite Convention">Tripartite Convention of 1899</a>, Germany ceded the Northern Solomon to Britain, minus Buka and Bougainville, the latter becoming part of <a href="/wiki/German_New_Guinea" title="German New Guinea">German New Guinea</a> despite geographically belonging to the Solomons archipelago.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Woodford's underfunded administration struggled to maintain law and order on the remote colony.<sup id="cite_ref-BRL6_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL6-12">[12]</a></sup> In the 1890s/early 1900s there were numerous cases of European settlers being killed by islanders, with the British often retaliating via collective punishment of guilty villages, often by indiscriminately shelling coastal areas from gunboats.<sup id="cite_ref-BRL6_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL6-12">[12]</a></sup> The British attempted to encourage plantation settlements, however by 1902 there only about 80 European settlers in the islands.<sup id="cite_ref-BRL9-1_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL9-1-46">[46]</a></sup> Attempts at economic development met with mixed results, though <a href="/wiki/Levers_Pacific_Plantations" title="Levers Pacific Plantations">Levers Pacific Plantations</a> Ltd., a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Lever_Brothers" title="Lever Brothers">Lever Brothers</a>, managed to establish a profitable copra plantation industry which employed many islanders.<sup id="cite_ref-BRL9-1_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRL9-1-46">[46]</a></sup> Small scale mining and logging industries were also developed.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> However the colony remained something of backwater, with education, medical and other social services being the preserve of the missionaries.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup> Violence also continued, most notably with the <a href="/wiki/Malaita_massacre" title="Malaita massacre">murder</a> of colonial administrator <a href="/wiki/William_R._Bell" title="William R. Bell">William R. Bell</a> by <a href="/wiki/Basiana" title="Basiana">Basiana</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kwaio_people" title="Kwaio people">Kwaio people</a> on Malaita in 1927, as Bell attempted to enforce an unpopular head tax. Several Kwaio were killed in a retaliatory raid, and Basiana and his accomplices executed.<sup id="cite_ref-WRB_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WRB-49">[49]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_campaign" title="Solomon Islands campaign">Solomon Islands campaign</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_the_Solomon_Islands" title="Japanese occupation of the Solomon Islands">Japanese occupation of the Solomon Islands</a>
      </div> 
      <p>From 1942 until the end of 1943, the Solomon Islands were the scene of several major land, sea and air battles between the Allies and the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Empire">Japanese Empire</a>'s armed forces.<sup id="cite_ref-NL_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NL-50">[50]</a></sup> Following the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbour" class="mw-redirect" title="Attack on Pearl Harbour">attack on Pearl Harbour</a> in 1941 war was declared between Japan and the Allied Powers, and the Japanese, seeking to protect their southern flank, invaded South-East Asia and New Guinea. In May 1942 the Japanese launched <a href="/wiki/Operation_Mo" title="Operation Mo">Operation Mo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Tulagi_(May_1942)" title="Invasion of Tulagi (May 1942)">occupying Tulagi</a> and most of the western Solomon Islands, including Guadalcanal where they began work on an airstrip.<sup id="cite_ref-ANU_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANU-51">[51]</a></sup> The British administration had already relocated to <a href="/wiki/Auki" title="Auki">Auki</a>, Malaita and most of the European population had been evacuated to Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-ANU_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANU-51">[51]</a></sup> The Allies <a href="/wiki/Guadalcanal_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Guadalcanal Campaign">counter-invaded Guadalcanal</a> in August 1942, followed by the <a href="/wiki/New_Georgia_campaign" title="New Georgia campaign">New Georgia campaign</a> in 1943, both of which were turning points in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a>, stopping and then countering the Japanese advance.<sup id="cite_ref-NL_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NL-50">[50]</a></sup> The conflict resulted in thousands of Allied, Japanese and civilian deaths, as well an immense destruction across the islands.<sup id="cite_ref-NL_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NL-50">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Coastwatchers" title="Coastwatchers">Coastwatchers</a> from the Solomon Islands played a major role in providing intelligence and rescuing other Allied servicemen.<sup id="cite_ref-ANU_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANU-51">[51]</a></sup> U.S. Admiral <a href="/wiki/William_Halsey" class="mw-redirect" title="William Halsey">William Halsey</a>, the commander of Allied forces during the Battle for Guadalcanal, recognised the coastwatchers' contributions by stating "The coastwatchers saved Guadalcanal and Guadalcanal saved the South Pacific."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In addition around 3,200 men served in the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_Labour_Corps" title="Solomon Islands Labour Corps">Solomon Islands Labour Corps</a> and some 6,000 enlisted in the <a href="/wiki/British_Solomon_Islands_Protectorate_Defence_Force" title="British Solomon Islands Protectorate Defence Force">British Solomon Islands Protectorate Defence Force</a>, with their exposure to the Americans leading to several social and political transformations.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> For example, the Americans had extensively developed <a href="/wiki/Honiara" title="Honiara">Honiara</a>, with the capital shifting there from Tulagi in 1952, and the <a href="/wiki/Pijin_language" title="Pijin language">Pijin language</a> was heavily influenced by the communication between Americans and the Islands inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The relatively easy-going, friendly attitude of the Americans also contrasted sharply with the subservience expected by the British colonial rulers, and profoundly changed Solomons Islanders' attitude to the colonial regime.<sup id="cite_ref-ANU-C4_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANU-C4-55">[55]</a></sup> </p>

      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_period_and_the_lead-up_to_independence">Post-war period and the lead-up to independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Post-war period and the lead-up to independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>In 1943–4 the Malaita-based chief Aliki Nono'ohimae had founded the <a href="/wiki/Maasina_Ruru" title="Maasina Ruru">Maasina Rule</a> movement (aka the Native Council Movement, literally 'Brotherhood Rule'), and was later joined by another chief, Hoasihau.<sup id="cite_ref-Maasina_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maasina-56">[56]</a></sup> Their aims were to improve the economic well-being of native Solomon Islanders, gain greater autonomy and to act as a liaison between Islanders and the colonial administration.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ANU-C4_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANU-C4-55">[55]</a></sup> The movement was especially popular with ex-Labour Corp members and after the war its numbers swelled, with the movement spreading to other islands.<sup id="cite_ref-ANU-C4_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANU-C4-55">[55]</a></sup> Alarmed at the growth of the movement, the British launched "Operation De-Louse" in 1947–8 and arrested most of the Maasina leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-ANU-C4_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ANU-C4-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maasina_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maasina-56">[56]</a></sup> Malaitans then organised a campaign of civil disobedience, prompting mass arrests.<sup id="cite_ref-Maasina_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maasina-56">[56]</a></sup> In 1950 a new Resident Commissioner, Henry Gregory-Smith, arrived and released the leaders of the movement, though the disobedience campaign continued.<sup id="cite_ref-Maasina_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maasina-56">[56]</a></sup> In 1952 new High Commissioner (later Governor) <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christopher_Stafford_Stanley" title="Robert Christopher Stafford Stanley">Robert Stanley</a> met with leaders of the movement and agreed to the creation of an island council.<sup id="cite_ref-Maasina_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maasina-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In late 1952 Stanley formally moved the capital of the territory to Honiara.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> In the early 1950s the possibility of transferring sovereignty of the islands to Australia was discussed by the British and Australian governments, however the Australians were reluctant to accept the financial burden of administraing the territory and the idea was shelved.<sup id="cite_ref-thompson_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thompson-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-goldsworthy_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goldsworthy-60">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>With decolonisation sweeping the colonial world, and Britain no longer willing (or able) to bear the financial burdens of the Empire, the colonial authorities sought to prepare the Solomons for self-governance. Appointed Executive and <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Council_of_the_Solomon_Islands" title="Legislative Council of the Solomon Islands">Legislative Councils</a> were established in 1960, with a degree of elected Solomon Islander representation introduced in 1964 and then extended in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> A new constitution was drawn up in 1970 which merged the two Councils into one <a href="/wiki/Governing_Council_of_the_Solomon_Islands" title="Governing Council of the Solomon Islands">Governing Council</a>, though the British Governor still retained extensive powers.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Discontent with this prompted the creation of a new constitution in 1974 which reduced much of the Governor's remaining powers and created the post of Chief Minister, first held by <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Mamaloni" title="Solomon Mamaloni">Solomon Mamaloni</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ind._64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind.-64">[64]</a></sup> Full self-government for the territory was achieved in 1976, a year after the independence of neighbouring Papua New Guinea from Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_38-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-38">[38]</a></sup> Meanwhile, discontent grew in the Western islands, with many fearing marginalisation in future a Honiara- or Malaita-dominated state, prompting the formation of the Western Breakaway Movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Ind._64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind.-64">[64]</a></sup> A conference held in London in 1977 agreed that the Solomons would gain full independence the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-Ind._64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ind.-64">[64]</a></sup> Under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_Act_1978" title="Solomon Islands Act 1978">Solomon Islands Act 1978</a> the country was annexed to <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">Her Majesty's dominions</a> and granted independence on 7 July 1978. The first Prime Minister was Sir <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kenilorea" title="Peter Kenilorea">Peter Kenilorea</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_United_Party" title="Solomon Islands United Party">Solomon Islands United Party</a> (SIUP), with <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> becoming <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Solomon_Islands" title="Monarchy of Solomon Islands">Queen of Solomon Islands</a>, represented locally by a <a href="/wiki/Governor-General_of_Solomon_Islands" title="Governor-General of Solomon Islands">Governor General</a>. </p>

         <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
          <div style="margin:44.5px auto;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:The_Five_Dollar_Proof_Coin_of_the_Solomon_Islands_24_October_1977.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/The_Five_Dollar_Proof_Coin_of_the_Solomon_Islands_24_October_1977.jpg/120px-The_Five_Dollar_Proof_Coin_of_the_Solomon_Islands_24_October_1977.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="61" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/The_Five_Dollar_Proof_Coin_of_the_Solomon_Islands_24_October_1977.jpg/180px-The_Five_Dollar_Proof_Coin_of_the_Solomon_Islands_24_October_1977.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/The_Five_Dollar_Proof_Coin_of_the_Solomon_Islands_24_October_1977.jpg/240px-The_Five_Dollar_Proof_Coin_of_the_Solomon_Islands_24_October_1977.jpg 2x" data-file-width="874" data-file-height="446"></a>
          </div>
         </div> 
         <div class="gallerytext"> 
          <p>The Five Dollar Proof Coin of the Solomon Islands 24 October 1977 </p> 
         </div> 
        </div></li> 
      </ul> 
      <h3><span id="Independence_era_.281978-present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_era_(1978-present)">Independence era (1978-present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Independence era (1978-present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_post-independence_years">Early post-independence years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early post-independence years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>Peter Kenilorea went on to win the <a href="/wiki/1980_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="1980 Solomon Islands general election">1980 Solomon Islands general election</a>, serving as PM until 1981, when he was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Mamaloni" title="Solomon Mamaloni">Solomon Mamaloni</a> of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Alliance_Party_(Solomon_Islands)" title="People's Alliance Party (Solomon Islands)">People's Alliance Party</a> (PAP) after a no confidence vote.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenilorea_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenilorea-65">[65]</a></sup> Mamaloni created the Central Bank and national airline, and pushed for greater autonomy for individual islands of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Mamaloni_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mamaloni-66">[66]</a></sup> Kenilorea returned to power after winning the <a href="/wiki/1984_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="1984 Solomon Islands general election">1984 election</a>, though his second term lasted only two years before he was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Ezekiel_Alebua" title="Ezekiel Alebua">Ezekiel Alebua</a> following allegations of misuse of French aid money.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In 1986 the Solomons helped found the <a href="/wiki/Melanesian_Spearhead_Group" title="Melanesian Spearhead Group">Melanesian Spearhead Group</a>, aimed at fostering cooperation and trade in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-MSGSec_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MSGSec-69">[69]</a></sup> After winning the <a href="/wiki/1989_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1989 election">1989 election</a> Mamaloni and the PAP returned to power, with Mamaloni dominating Solomon Islands politics from the early to mid 1990s (save for the one year Premiership of <a href="/wiki/Francis_Billy_Hilly" title="Francis Billy Hilly">Francis Billy Hilly</a>). Mamaloni made efforts to make the Solomons a republic, however these were unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-Mamaloni_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mamaloni-66">[66]</a></sup> He also had to deal with the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Bougainville_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Bougainville Civil War">conflict</a> in neighbouring Bougainville which broke out in 1988, causing many refugees to flee to the Solomons.<sup id="cite_ref-Situ_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Situ-70">[70]</a></sup> Tensions arose with Papua New Guinea as PNG forces frequently entered Solomons territory in the pursuit of rebels.<sup id="cite_ref-Situ_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Situ-70">[70]</a></sup> The situation calmed down and relations improved following the end of the conflict in 1998. Meanwhile, the country's financial situation continued to deteriorate, with much of the budget coming from the logging industry, often conducted at an unsustainable rate, not helped by Mamaloni's creation of a 'discretionary fund' for use by politicians, which fostered fraud and corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-Mamaloni_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mamaloni-66">[66]</a></sup> Discontent with his rule led to a split in the PAP, and Mamaloni lost the <a href="/wiki/1993_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="1993 Solomon Islands general election">1993 election</a> to Billy Hilly, though Hilly was later sacked by the Governor-General after a number of defections caused him to lose his majority, allowing Mamloni to return to power in 1994, where he remained until 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-Mamaloni_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mamaloni-66">[66]</a></sup> Excessive logging, government corruption and unsustainable levels of public spending continued to grow, and public discontent caused Mamaloni to lose the <a href="/wiki/1997_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="1997 Solomon Islands general election">1997 election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mamaloni_66-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mamaloni-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup> The new Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Bartholomew_Ulufa%27alu" title="Bartholomew Ulufa'alu">Bartholomew Ulufa'alu</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands_Liberal_Party" title="Solomon Islands Liberal Party">Solomon Islands Liberal Party</a>, attempted to enact economic reforms, however his Premiership soon became engulfed in a serious ethnic conflict known as 'The Tensions'.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span id="Ethnic_violence_.281998.E2.80.932003.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_violence_(1998–2003)">Ethnic violence (1998–2003)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ethnic violence (1998–2003)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg/220px-Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg/330px-Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg/440px-Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1995"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Australian_soldiers_burning_guns_during_the_RAMSI_deployment_in_October_2003.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Australian troops, as part of the RAMSI peacekeeping mission, burn weapons confiscated from or surrendered by militias in 2003
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Commonly referred to as <i>the tensions</i> or <i>the ethnic tension</i>, the initial civil unrest was mainly characterised by fighting between the <a href="/wiki/Isatabu_Freedom_Movement" title="Isatabu Freedom Movement">Isatabu Freedom Movement</a> (IFM, also known as the Guadalcanal Revolutionary Army and the Isatabu Freedom Fighters) and the <a href="/wiki/Malaita_Eagle_Force" title="Malaita Eagle Force">Malaita Eagle Force</a> (as well as the Marau Eagle Force).<sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup> For many years people from the island of Malaita had been migrating to Honiara and Guadalcanal, attracted primarily by the greater economic opportunities available there.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> The large influx caused tensions with native Guadalcanal islanders (known as Guales), and in late 1998 the IFM was formed and began a campaign of intimidation and violence towards Malaitan settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup> Thousands of Malaitans subsequently fled back to Malaita or to Honiara, and in mid-1999 the Malaita Eagle Force (MEF) was established to protect Malaitans on Guadalcanal.<sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup> In late 1999, after several failed attempts at brokering a peace deal, Prime Minister Bartholomew Ulufa'aluthe declared a four-month state of emergency, and also requested assistance from Australia and New Zealand, but his appeal was rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup> Meanwhile, law and order on Guadalcanal collapsed, with an ethnically divided police unable to assert authority and many of their weapons depots being raided by the militias; by this point the MEF controlled Honiara with the IFM controlling the rest of Guadalacanal.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 5 June 2000 Ulufa'alu was kidnapped by the MEF who felt that, although he was a Malaitan, he was not doing enough to protect their interests.<sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup> Ulufa'alu subsequently resigned in exchange for his release.<sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manasseh_Sogavare" title="Manasseh Sogavare">Manasseh Sogavare</a>, who had earlier been Finance Minister in Ulufa'alu's government but had subsequently joined the opposition, was elected as Prime Minister by 23–21 over the Rev. <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Boseto" title="Leslie Boseto">Leslie Boseto</a>. However, Sogavare's election was immediately shrouded in controversy because six MPs (thought to be supporters of Boseto) were unable to attend parliament for the crucial vote.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> On 15 October 2000 the <a href="/wiki/Townsville_Peace_Agreement" title="Townsville Peace Agreement">Townsville Peace Agreement</a> was signed by the MEF, elements of the IFM, and the Solomon Islands Government.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup> This was closely followed by the Marau Peace agreement in February 2001, signed by the Marau Eagle Force, the IFM, the Guadalcanal Provincial Government, and the Solomon Islands Government.<sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup> However, a key Guale militant leader, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Keke" title="Harold Keke">Harold Keke</a>, refused to sign the agreement, causing a split with the Guale groups.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> Subsequently, Guale signatories to the agreement led by Andrew Te'e joined with the Malaitan-dominated police to form the 'Joint Operations Force'.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> During the next two years the conflict moved to the remote Weathercoast region of southern Guadalcanal as the Joint Operations unsuccessfully attempted to capture Keke and his group.<sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_(10709848893).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_%2810709848893%29.jpg/220px-New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_%2810709848893%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_%2810709848893%29.jpg/330px-New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_%2810709848893%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_%2810709848893%29.jpg/440px-New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_%2810709848893%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2837" data-file-height="1892"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:New_beginning_for_Solomon_Islands_(10709848893).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Solomon Islanders at a peace protest in 2003
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>By early 2001 the economy had collapsed and the government was bankrupt.<sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup> New <a href="/wiki/2001_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="2001 Solomon Islands general election">elections</a> in December 2001 brought <a href="/wiki/Allan_Kemakeza" title="Allan Kemakeza">Allan Kemakeza</a> into the Prime Minister's chair, with the support of his People's Alliance Party and the Association of Independent Members. Law and order deteriorated as the nature of the conflict shifted: there was continuing violence on the Weathercoast, whilst militants in Honiara increasingly turned their attention to crime, extortion and banditry.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> The Department of Finance would often be surrounded by armed men when funding was due to arrive. In December 2002, Finance Minister <a href="/wiki/Laurie_Chan" title="Laurie Chan">Laurie Chan</a> resigned after being forced at gunpoint to sign a cheque made out to some of the militants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Conflict also broke out in Western Province between locals and Malaitan settlers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Renegade members of the <a href="/wiki/Bougainville_Revolutionary_Army" title="Bougainville Revolutionary Army">Bougainville Revolutionary Army</a> (BRA) were invited in as a protection force but ended up causing as much trouble as they prevented.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> The prevailing atmosphere of lawlessness, widespread extortion, and ineffective police prompted a formal request by the Solomon Islands Government for outside help, a request was unanimously supported in Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In July 2003, Australian and Pacific Islands police and troops arrived in Solomon Islands under the auspices of the Australian-led <a href="/wiki/Regional_Assistance_Mission_to_Solomon_Islands" title="Regional Assistance Mission to Solomon Islands">Regional Assistance Mission to Solomon Islands</a> (RAMSI).<sup id="cite_ref-RAMSI_73-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAMSI-73">[73]</a></sup> A sizeable international security contingent of 2,200 police and troops, led by Australia and New Zealand, and with representatives from about 15 other Pacific nations, began arriving the next month under Operation Helpem Fren.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> The situation improved dramatically, with violence ending and Harold Keke surrendering to the force.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-77">[77]</a></sup> Some 200 people had been killed in the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> Since this time some commentators have considered the country a <a href="/wiki/Failed_state" title="Failed state">failed state</a>, with the nation having failed to build an inclusive national identity capable of overriding local island and ethnic loyalties.<sup id="cite_ref-TTK_71-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TTK-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> However, other academics argue that rather than being a 'failed state', it is an unformed state: a state that never consolidated even after decades of independence.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Furthermore, some scholars, such Kabutaulaka (2001) and Dinnen (2002) argue that the 'ethnic conflict' label is an oversimplification.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-conflict_era">Post-conflict era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Post-conflict era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>Kemakeza remained in office until April 2006, when he lost the <a href="/wiki/2006_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="2006 Solomon Islands general election">2006 Solomon Islands general election</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snyder_Rini" title="Snyder Rini">Snyder Rini</a> became PM. However allegations that Rini had used bribes from Chinese businessmen to buy the votes of members of Parliament led to mass rioting in the capital <a href="/wiki/Honiara" title="Honiara">Honiara</a>, concentrated on the city's Chinatown area. A deep underlying resentment against the minority <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Chinese</a> business community led to much of <a href="/wiki/Chinatown" title="Chinatown">Chinatown</a> in the city being destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-81">[81]</a></sup> Tensions were also increased by the belief that large sums of money were being exported to China. China sent chartered aircraft to evacuate hundreds of Chinese who fled to avoid the riots.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Evacuation of Australian and British citizens was on a much smaller scale.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Additional Australian, New Zealand and Fijian police and troops were dispatched to try to quell the unrest. Rini eventually resigned before facing a motion of no-confidence in Parliament, and Parliament elected <a href="/wiki/Manasseh_Sogavare" title="Manasseh Sogavare">Manasseh Sogavare</a> as Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-NZ_Herald_10379008_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_Herald_10379008-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Sogavare struggled to assert his authority and was also hostile to the Australian presence in the country; after one failed attempt, he was removed in no confidence vote in 2007 and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Derek_Sikua" title="Derek Sikua">Derek Sikua</a> of the Solomon Islands Liberal Party.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In 2008 a <a href="/wiki/Truth_and_Reconciliation_Commission_(Solomon_Islands)" title="Truth and Reconciliation Commission (Solomon Islands)">Truth and Reconciliation Commission</a> was established to examine and help heal the wounds of the 'tension' years.<sup id="cite_ref-st_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-st-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rnz_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnz-86">[86]</a></sup> Sikua lost the <a href="/wiki/2010_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="2010 Solomon Islands general election">2010 Solomon Islands general election</a> to <a href="/wiki/Danny_Philip" title="Danny Philip">Danny Philip</a>, though after a vote of no confidence in him following allegations of corruption, Philip was ousted and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Darcy_Lilo" title="Gordon Darcy Lilo">Gordon Darcy Lilo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-solomonstar3_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-solomonstar3-88">[88]</a></sup> Sogavare returned to power after the <a href="/wiki/2014_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="2014 Solomon Islands general election">2014 election</a>, and oversaw the withdrawal of RAMSI forces from the country in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-SMH_74-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SMH-74">[74]</a></sup> Sogavare was ousted in a no confidence vote in 2017, which saw <a href="/wiki/Rick_Houenipwela" title="Rick Houenipwela">Rick Houenipwela</a> come to power, however Sogavare returned to the Prime Ministership after winning the <a href="/wiki/2019_Solomon_Islands_general_election" title="2019 Solomon Islands general election">2019 election</a>, sparking rioting in Honiara.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In 2019 Sogavare announced that the Solomons would be switching recognition from Taiwan to China.<sup id="cite_ref-rnz.co.nz_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rnz.co.nz-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Solomon_Islands&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Solomon_Islands" title="Politics of Solomon Islands">Politics of Solomon Islands</a>
      </div>
